<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=GlaxoSmithKline&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Glaxo_Wellcome">Glaxo Wellcome</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=GlaxoSmithKline&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Glaxo Wellcome">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Glaxo_Factory,_Bunnythorpe,_New_Zealand_03.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3216" data-file-width="4288" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Glaxo_Factory%2C_Bunnythorpe%2C_New_Zealand_03.JPG/220px-Glaxo_Factory%2C_Bunnythorpe%2C_New_Zealand_03.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Glaxo_Factory%2C_Bunnythorpe%2C_New_Zealand_03.JPG/330px-Glaxo_Factory%2C_Bunnythorpe%2C_New_Zealand_03.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Glaxo_Factory%2C_Bunnythorpe%2C_New_Zealand_03.JPG/440px-Glaxo_Factory%2C_Bunnythorpe%2C_New_Zealand_03.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Glaxo_Factory,_Bunnythorpe,_New_Zealand_03.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The historic Glaxo factory in <a href="/wiki/Bunnythorpe" title="Bunnythorpe">Bunnythorpe, New Zealand</a>, with the Glaxo Laboratories sign still visible</div></div></div>
<p>Joseph Nathan and Co. was founded in 1873 as a general trading company in <a href="/wiki/Wellington" title="Wellington">Wellington</a>, New Zealand, by a Londoner, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joseph_Nathan_(businessman)" title="Joseph Nathan (businessman)">Joseph Edward Nathan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[11]</a></sup> In 1904 it began producing a dried-milk baby food from excess milk produced on the family farms outside <a href="/wiki/Bunnythorpe" title="Bunnythorpe">Bunnythorpe</a>. The resulting product was first known as Defiance, then as Glaxo (from <i>lacto</i>), under the slogan "Glaxo builds bonny babies."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ravenscraft2000_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ravenscraft2000-14">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>306</span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-history-15">[14]</a></sup> The Glaxo Laboratories sign is still visible <i>(right)</i> on what is now a car repair shop on the main street of Bunnythorpe. The company's first pharmaceutical product, released in 1924, was vitamin D.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ravenscraft2000_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ravenscraft2000-14">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>306</span></sup>
</p><p>Glaxo Laboratories was registered as a subsidiary company in London in 1935. Glaxo bought out its parent, Joseph Nathan in 1947 and acquired <a href="/wiki/Allen_%26_Hanburys" title="Allen &amp; Hanburys">Allen &amp; Hanburys</a> in 1958. The Scottish pharmacologist <a href="/wiki/David_Jack_(scientist)" title="David Jack (scientist)">David Jack</a> was hired as a researcher for Allen &amp; Hanburys a few years after Glaxo took it over; he went on to lead the company's R&amp;D until 1987.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ravenscraft2000_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ravenscraft2000-14">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>306</span></sup> After the company bought Meyer Laboratories in 1978, it began to play an important role in the US market. In 1983 the American arm, Glaxo Inc., moved to <a href="/wiki/Research_Triangle_Park" title="Research Triangle Park">Research Triangle Park</a> (US headquarters/research) and Zebulon (US manufacturing) in North Carolina.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-history-15">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>Burroughs Wellcome &amp; Company was founded in 1880 in London by the American pharmacists <a href="/wiki/Henry_Wellcome" title="Henry Wellcome">Henry Wellcome</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silas_Mainville_Burroughs_(pharmacist)" title="Silas Mainville Burroughs (pharmacist)">Silas Burroughs</a>. The Wellcome Tropical Research Laboratories opened in 1902. In the 1920s Burroughs Wellcome established research and manufacturing facilities in <a href="/wiki/Tuckahoe_(village),_New_York" title="Tuckahoe (village), New York">Tuckahoe, New York</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TriCent_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-TriCent-16">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>18</span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup> which served as the US headquarters until the company moved to <a href="/wiki/Research_Triangle_Park" title="Research Triangle Park">Research Triangle Park</a> in North Carolina in 1971.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup> The Nobel Prize winning scientists <a href="/wiki/Gertrude_B._Elion" title="Gertrude B. Elion">Gertrude B. Elion</a> and <a href="/wiki/George_H._Hitchings" title="George H. Hitchings">George H. Hitchings</a> worked there and invented drugs still used many years later, such as <a href="/wiki/Mercaptopurine" title="Mercaptopurine">mercaptopurine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup> In 1959 the Wellcome Foundation bought <a href="/wiki/William_Cooper_(chemical_manufacturer)" title="William Cooper (chemical manufacturer)">Cooper, McDougall &amp; Robertson Inc</a> to become more active in animal health.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-history-15">[14]</a></sup> Glaxo and Wellcome merged in 1995 to form Glaxo Wellcome plc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ravenscraft2000_14-3"><a href="#cite_note-Ravenscraft2000-14">[13]</a></sup> Glaxo Wellcome restructured its R&amp;D operation that year, cutting 10,000 jobs worldwide, closing its R&amp;D facility in Beckenham, Kent, and opening a Medicines Research Centre in Stevenage, Hertfordshire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup> Also that year, Glaxo Wellcome acquired the California-based Affymax, a leader in the field of <a href="/wiki/Combinatorial_chemistry" title="Combinatorial chemistry">combinatorial chemistry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>By 1999 Glaxo Wellcome had become the world's third-largest pharmaceutical company by revenues (behind <a href="/wiki/Novartis" title="Novartis">Novartis</a> and Merck), with a global market share of around 4 per cent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup> Its products included <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imigran" title="Imigran">Imigran</a> (for the treatment of migraine), <a href="/wiki/Salbutamol" title="Salbutamol">salbutamol</a> (Ventolin) (for the treatment of asthma), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zovirax" title="Zovirax">Zovirax</a> (for the treatment of coldsores), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retrovir" title="Retrovir">Retrovir</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epivir" title="Epivir">Epivir</a> (for the treatment of AIDS). In 1999 the company was the world's largest manufacturer of drugs for the treatment of asthma and HIV/AIDS.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-indep1899_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-indep1899-27">[26]</a></sup> It employed 59,000 people, including 13,400 in the UK, had 76 operating companies and 50 manufacturing facilities worldwide, and seven of its products were among the world's top 50 best-selling pharmaceuticals. The company had R&amp;D facilities in Hertfordshire, Kent, London and Verona (Italy), and manufacturing plants in Scotland and the north of England. It had R&amp;D centres in the US and Japan, and production facilities in the US, Europe and the Far East.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc17100_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc17100-28">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="SmithKline_Beecham">SmithKline Beecham</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=GlaxoSmithKline&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: SmithKline Beecham">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BeechamsBuilding.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1212" data-file-width="1118" height="238" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/BeechamsBuilding.jpg/220px-BeechamsBuilding.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/BeechamsBuilding.jpg/330px-BeechamsBuilding.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/BeechamsBuilding.jpg/440px-BeechamsBuilding.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BeechamsBuilding.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Beecham's Clock Tower, constructed 1877, part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beecham_(pharmaceutical_company)" title="Beecham (pharmaceutical company)">Beecham</a>'s factory, <a href="/wiki/St_Helens,_Merseyside" title="St Helens, Merseyside">St Helens</a></div></div></div>
<p>In 1848 <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Beecham_(chemist)" title="Thomas Beecham (chemist)">Thomas Beecham</a> launched his <a href="/wiki/Beecham%27s_Pills" title="Beecham's Pills">Beecham's Pills</a> laxative in England, giving birth to the <a href="/wiki/Beecham_Group" title="Beecham Group">Beecham Group</a>. In 1859 Beecham opened its first factory in <a href="/wiki/St_Helens,_Merseyside" title="St Helens, Merseyside">St Helens</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lancashire" title="Lancashire">Lancashire</a>. By the 1960s Beecham was extensively involved in pharmaceuticals and consumer products such as Macleans toothpaste, Lucozade and synthetic penicillin research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-history-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/John_K._Smith" title="John K. Smith">John K. Smith</a> opened his first pharmacy in Philadelphia in 1830. In 1865 <a href="/wiki/Mahlon_Kline" title="Mahlon Kline">Mahlon Kline</a> joined the business, which 10 years later became Smith, Kline &amp; Co. In 1891 it merged with French, Richard and Company, and in 1929 changed its name to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SmithKline_%26_French" title="SmithKline &amp; French">Smith Kline &amp; French Laboratories</a> as it focused more on research. Years later it bought Norden Laboratories, a business doing research into animal health, and <a href="/wiki/Recherche_et_Industrie_Th%C3%A9rapeutiques" title="Recherche et Industrie Thérapeutiques">Recherche et Industrie Thérapeutiques</a> in Belgium in 1963 to focus on vaccines. The company began to expand globally, buying seven laboratories in Canada and the United States in 1969. In 1982 it bought <a href="/wiki/Allergan" title="Allergan">Allergan</a>, a manufacturer of eye and skincare products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history_15-4"><a href="#cite_note-history-15">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>SmithKline &amp; French merged with Beckman Inc. in 1982 and changed its name to <i>SmithKline Beckman</i>. In 1988 it bought its biggest competitor, International Clinical Laboratories, and in 1989 merged with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beecham_(pharmaceutical_company)" title="Beecham (pharmaceutical company)">Beecham</a> to form <i>SmithKline Beecham plc</i>. The headquarters moved from the United States to England. To expand R&amp;D in the United States, the company bought a new research center in 1995; another opened in 1997 in England at New Frontiers Science Park, <a href="/wiki/Harlow" title="Harlow">Harlow</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history_15-5"><a href="#cite_note-history-15">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2000:_Glaxo_Wellcome_and_SmithKline_Beecham_merger">2000: Glaxo Wellcome and SmithKline Beecham merger</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=GlaxoSmithKline&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 2000: Glaxo Wellcome and SmithKline Beecham merger">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Glaxo Wellcome and SmithKline Beecham announced their intention to merge in January 2000. The merger was completed in December that year, forming GlaxoSmithKline (GSK).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[30]</a></sup> The company's global headquarters are at GSK House, <a href="/wiki/Brentford" title="Brentford">Brentford</a>, London, officially opened in 2002 by then-Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Tony_Blair" title="Tony Blair">Tony Blair</a>. The building was erected at a cost of £300 million and as of 2002 was home to 3,000 administrative staff.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tele15702_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-tele15702-32">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="2001.E2.80.932010"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="2001–2010">2001–2010</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=GlaxoSmithKline&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 2001–2010">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Andrew_Witty_in_2015.jpg"><img alt="photograph" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3872" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Andrew_Witty_in_2015.jpg/220px-Andrew_Witty_in_2015.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Andrew_Witty_in_2015.jpg/330px-Andrew_Witty_in_2015.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Andrew_Witty_in_2015.jpg/440px-Andrew_Witty_in_2015.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Andrew_Witty_in_2015.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Andrew_Witty" title="Andrew Witty">Andrew Witty</a>, GSK's CEO from May 2008 to April 2017</div></div></div>
<p>GSK completed the acquisition of New Jersey-based <a href="/wiki/Block_Drug" title="Block Drug">Block Drug</a> in 2001 for US$1.24 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup> In 2006 GSK acquired the US-based consumer healthcare company CNS Inc., whose products included Breathe Right nasal strips and FiberChoice dietary supplements, for US$566 million in cash.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Chris_Gent" title="Chris Gent">Chris Gent</a>, previously CEO of <a href="/wiki/Vodafone" title="Vodafone">Vodafone</a>, was appointed chairman of the board in 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup> GSK opened its first R&amp;D centre in China in 2007, in Shanghai, initially focused on neurodegenerative diseases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Witty" title="Andrew Witty">Andrew Witty</a> became the chief executive officer in 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[36]</a></sup> Witty joined Glaxo in 1985 and had been president of GSK's Pharmaceuticals Europe since 2003.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2009 GSK acquired <a href="/wiki/Stiefel_Laboratories" title="Stiefel Laboratories">Stiefel Laboratories</a>, then the world's largest independent dermatology drug company, for US$3.6bn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup> In November the FDA approved GSK's vaccine for 2009 H1N1 influenza protection, manufactured by the company's ID Biomedical Corp in Canada.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup> Also in November 2009 GSK formed a joint venture with <a href="/wiki/Pfizer" title="Pfizer">Pfizer</a> to create <a href="/wiki/ViiV_Healthcare" title="ViiV Healthcare">ViiV Healthcare</a>, which specializes in HIV research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[40]</a></sup> In 2010 the company acquired Laboratorios Phoenix, an Argentine pharmaceutical company, for US$253m,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[41]</a></sup> and the UK-based sports nutrition company Maxinutrition for £162 million (US$256 million).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="2011.E2.80.93present"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="2011–present">2011–present</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=GlaxoSmithKline&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 2011–present">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2011, in a $660-million deal, <a href="/wiki/Prestige_Brands" title="Prestige Brands">Prestige Brands Holdings</a> took over 17 GSK brands with sales of $210 million, including <a href="/wiki/BC_Powder" title="BC Powder">BC Powder</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beano_(dietary_supplement)" title="Beano (dietary supplement)">Beano</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecotrin" title="Ecotrin">Ecotrin</a>, Fiber Choice, <a href="/wiki/Goody%27s_Powder" title="Goody's Powder">Goody's Powder</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sominex" title="Sominex">Sominex</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tagamet" title="Tagamet">Tagamet</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[43]</a></sup> In 2012 the company announced that it would invest £500 million in manufacturing facilities in <a href="/wiki/Ulverston" title="Ulverston">Ulverston</a>, northern England, designating it as the site for a previously announced biotech plant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[44]</a></sup> In May that year it acquired CellZome, a German biotech company, for US$98 million,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[45]</a></sup> and in June worldwide rights to <a href="/wiki/Alitretinoin" title="Alitretinoin">alitretinoin</a> (Toctino), an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eczema" title="Eczema">eczema</a> drug, for $302 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[46]</a></sup> In 2013 GSK acquired <a href="/wiki/Human_Genome_Sciences" title="Human Genome Sciences">Human Genome Sciences</a> (HGS) for $3 billion; the companies had collaborated on developing the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lupus" title="Lupus">lupus</a> drug <a href="/wiki/Belimumab" title="Belimumab">Belimumab</a> (Benlysta), <a href="/wiki/Albiglutide" title="Albiglutide">albiglutide</a> for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Type_2_diabetes" title="Type 2 diabetes">type 2 diabetes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Darapladib" title="Darapladib">darapladib</a> for <a href="/wiki/Atherosclerosis" title="Atherosclerosis">atherosclerosis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3Lessons_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-3Lessons-48">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>In March 2014, GSK paid $1 billion to raise its stake in its Indian pharmaceutical unit, GlaxoSmithKline Pharmaceuticals, to 75 percent as part of a move to focus on emerging markets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[48]</a></sup> In April 2014 Novartis and Glaxo agreed on more than $20 billion in deals, with Novartis selling its vaccine business to GSK and buying GSK's cancer business.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wsj4222014_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-wsj4222014-51">[50]</a></sup> In February 2015 GSK announced that it would acquire GlycoVaxyn, a Swiss pharmaceutical company, for $190 million,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup> and in June that year that it would sell two <a href="/wiki/Meningitis" title="Meningitis">meningitis</a> drugs to <a href="/wiki/Pfizer" title="Pfizer">Pfizer</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Nimenrix&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Nimenrix (page does not exist)">Nimenrix</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mencevax" title="Mencevax">Mencevax</a> for around $130 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Philip_Hampton" title="Philip Hampton">Philip Hampton</a>, at that time chair of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Bank_of_Scotland" title="Royal Bank of Scotland">Royal Bank of Scotland</a>, became GSK chairman in September 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>In September 2016, the company announced that Witty would be succeeded as CEO by <a href="/wiki/Emma_Walmsley" title="Emma Walmsley">Emma Walmsley</a> in March 2017; Walmsley was a management professional originally from Lancashire with a background in marketing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-successionAWtoEW_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-successionAWtoEW-55">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>In December 2017, Reuters reported that Glaxo had increased its stake in its Saudi Arabian unit to 75% (from 49%) taking over control from its Saudi partner <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Banaja_KSA_Holding_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Banaja KSA Holding Company (page does not exist)">Banaja KSA Holding Company</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>In March 2018, GSK announced that it has reached an agreement with <a href="/wiki/Novartis" title="Novartis">Novartis</a> to acquire Novartis’ 36.5% stake in their Consumer Healthcare Joint Venture for $13 billion (£9.2 billion).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup> This acquisition is consistent with the notion prevalent by July 2018 that the company was seriously considering a breakup that would spin-off the company's Consumer division.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-58">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>With respect to <a href="/wiki/Rare_disease" title="Rare disease">rare diseases</a>, the company divested its portfolio of <a href="/wiki/Gene_therapy" title="Gene therapy">gene therapy</a> drugs to Orchard Therapeutics in April 2018.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-58">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Venture_arms">Venture arms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=GlaxoSmithKline&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Venture arms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>SR One was established in 1985 by SmithKline Beecham to invest in new biotechnology companies and continued operating after GSK was formed; by 2003 GSK had formed another subsidiary, GSK Ventures, to out-license or start new companies around drug candidates that it did not intend to develop further.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JPE2003_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-JPE2003-59">[58]</a></sup>  As of 2003, SR One tended to invest only if the company aligned with GSK's business.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JPE2003_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-JPE2003-59">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Research_areas_and_products">Research areas and products</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=GlaxoSmithKline&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Research areas and products">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_GlaxoSmithKline_products" title="List of GlaxoSmithKline products">List of GlaxoSmithKline products</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pharmaceuticals">Pharmaceuticals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=GlaxoSmithKline&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Pharmaceuticals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>GSK manufactures products for major disease areas such as asthma, cancer, infections, diabetes and mental health. Its biggest-selling products in 2013 were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seretide" title="Seretide">Advair</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Avodart" title="Avodart">Avodart</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flixotide" title="Flixotide">Flovent</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Augmentin" title="Augmentin">Augmentin</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lovaza" title="Lovaza">Lovaza</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lamictal" title="Lamictal">Lamictal</a>; its drugs and vaccines earned £21.3 billion that year. Other top-selling products include its <a href="/wiki/Asthma" title="Asthma">asthma</a>/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/COPD" title="COPD">COPD</a> inhalers Advair, Ventolin, and Flovent; its <a href="/wiki/Diphtheria" title="Diphtheria">diphtheria</a>/<a href="/wiki/Tetanus" title="Tetanus">tetanus</a>/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pertussis" title="Pertussis">pertussis</a> vaccine Infanrix and its <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_B_vaccine" title="Hepatitis B vaccine">hepatitis B vaccine</a>; the epilepsy drug <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lamictal" title="Lamictal">Lamictal</a>, and the antibacterial Augmentin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AR2013_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-AR2013-8">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>220</span></sup>
</p><p>Medicines historically discovered or developed at GSK and its legacy companies and now sold as generics include <a href="/wiki/Amoxicillin" title="Amoxicillin">amoxicillin</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amoxicillin-clavulanate" title="Amoxicillin-clavulanate">amoxicillin-clavulanate</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[60]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ticarcillin/clavulanic_acid" title="Ticarcillin/clavulanic acid">ticarcillin-clavulanate</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[61]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mupirocin" title="Mupirocin">mupirocin</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Ceftazidime" title="Ceftazidime">ceftazidime</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup> for bacterial infections, <a href="/wiki/Zidovudine" title="Zidovudine">zidovudine</a> for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HIV_infection" title="HIV infection">HIV infection</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Valacyclovir" title="Valacyclovir">valacyclovir</a> for herpes virus infections, <a href="/wiki/Albendazole" title="Albendazole">albendazole</a> for parasitic infections, <a href="/wiki/Sumatriptan" title="Sumatriptan">sumatriptan</a> for <a href="/wiki/Migraine" title="Migraine">migraine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lamotrigine" title="Lamotrigine">lamotrigine</a> for epilepsy, <a href="/wiki/Bupropion" title="Bupropion">bupropion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paroxetine" title="Paroxetine">paroxetine</a> for <a href="/wiki/Major_depressive_disorder" title="Major depressive disorder">major depressive disorder</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cimetidine" title="Cimetidine">cimetidine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ranitidine" title="Ranitidine">ranitidine</a> for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gastroesophageal_reflux_disorder" title="Gastroesophageal reflux disorder">gastroesophageal reflux disorder</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mercaptopurine" title="Mercaptopurine">mercaptopurine</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pubs.acs.org_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-pubs.acs.org-65">[64]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thioguanine" title="Thioguanine">thioguanine</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ScienceHistory_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-ScienceHistory-66">[65]</a></sup> for the treatment of leukemia, <a href="/wiki/Allopurinol" title="Allopurinol">allopurinol</a> for <a href="/wiki/Gout" title="Gout">gout</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_purine_path_to_chemotherapy_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_purine_path_to_chemotherapy-67">[66]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pyrimethamine" title="Pyrimethamine">pyrimethamine</a> for <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">malaria</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes.com_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes.com-68">[67]</a></sup> and the antibacterial <a href="/wiki/Trimethoprim" title="Trimethoprim">trimethoprim</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ScienceHistory_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-ScienceHistory-66">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>Among these, albendazole, amoxicillin, amoxicillin-clavulanate, allopurinol, mercaptopurine, mupriocin, pyrimethamine, ranitidine, thioguanine, trimethoprim and zidovudine are listed on the World Health Organization's list of essential medications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Malaria_vaccine">Malaria vaccine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=GlaxoSmithKline&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Malaria vaccine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2014 GSK applied for regulatory approval for the first <a href="/wiki/Malaria_vaccine" title="Malaria vaccine">malaria vaccine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[69]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">Malaria</a> is responsible for over 650,000 deaths annually, mainly in Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[70]</a></sup> Known as <a href="/wiki/RTS,S" title="RTS,S">RTS,S</a>, the vaccine was developed as a joint project with the <a href="/wiki/PATH_(global_health_organization)" title="PATH (global health organization)">PATH</a> vaccines initiative and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bill_and_Melinda_Gates_Foundation" title="Bill and Melinda Gates Foundation">Bill and Melinda Gates Foundation</a>. The company has committed to making the vaccine available in developing countries for five percent above the cost of production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-malariavaccine_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-malariavaccine-10">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>As of 2013 RTS,S, which uses GSK's proprietary AS01 adjuvant, was being examined in a Phase 3 trial in eight African countries. PATH reported that "[i]n the 12-month period following vaccination, RTS,S conferred approximately 50% protection from clinical Plasmodium falciparum disease in children aged 5-17 months, and approximately 30% protection in children aged 6-12 weeks when administered in conjunction with Expanded Program for Immunization (EPI) vaccines."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[71]</a></sup> In 2014 Glaxo said it had spent more than $350 million and expected to spend an additional $260 million before seeking regulatory approval.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[73]</a></sup> A second generation malaria vaccine is being evaluated in Phase 2 clinical trials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[74]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Consumer_healthcare">Consumer healthcare</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=GlaxoSmithKline&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Consumer healthcare">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>GSK's consumer healthcare division, which earned £5.2 billion in 2013, sells oral healthcare, including  <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aquafresh" title="Aquafresh">Aquafresh</a>, Maclean's and <a href="/wiki/Sensodyne" title="Sensodyne">Sensodyne</a> toothpastes; and drinks such as <a href="/wiki/Horlicks" title="Horlicks">Horlicks</a>, Boost and a chocolate-flavoured malt drink sold in India. GSK also previously owned the <a href="/wiki/Lucozade" title="Lucozade">Lucozade</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ribena" title="Ribena">Ribena</a> brands of soft drinks, but they were sold in 2013 to <a href="/wiki/Suntory" title="Suntory">Suntory</a> for £1.35bn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Monaghan_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Monaghan-76">[75]</a></sup> Other products include <a href="/wiki/Docosanol" title="Docosanol">Abreva</a> to treat cold sores; Night Nurse, a cold remedy; Breathe Right <a href="/wiki/Nasal_strip" title="Nasal strip">nasal strips</a>; and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nicoderm" title="Nicoderm">Nicoderm</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nicorette" title="Nicorette">Nicorette</a> nicotine replacements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maj07_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Maj07-77">[76]</a></sup> In March 2014 it recalled <a href="/wiki/Orlistat" title="Orlistat">Alli</a>, an over-the-counter weight-loss drug, in the United States and <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico" title="Puerto Rico">Puerto Rico</a> because of possible tampering, following customer complaints.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Facilities">Facilities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=GlaxoSmithKline&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Facilities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As of 2013 GSK had offices in over 115 countries and employed over 99,000 people, 12,500 in <a href="/wiki/Research_and_development" title="Research and development">R&amp;D</a>. The company's single largest market is the United States. Its US headquarters are in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Navy_Yard" title="The Navy Yard">The Navy Yard</a>, Philadelphia, and <a href="/wiki/Research_Triangle_Park" title="Research Triangle Park">Research Triangle Park</a>, North Carolina; its consumer-products division is in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moon_Township,_Pennsylvania" title="Moon Township, Pennsylvania">Moon Township, Pennsylvania</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AR2013_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-AR2013-8">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>7</span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-achievements_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-achievements-79">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Recognition.2C_philanthropy_and_social_responsibility"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Recognition,_philanthropy_and_social_responsibility">Recognition, philanthropy and social responsibility</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=GlaxoSmithKline&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Recognition, philanthropy and social responsibility">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scientific_recognition">Scientific recognition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=GlaxoSmithKline&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Scientific recognition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Four GlaxoSmithKline scientists have been recognized by the Nobel Committee for their contributions to basic medical science and/or therapeutics development.
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Henry_Hallett_Dale" title="Henry Hallett Dale">Henry Dale</a>, a former student of <a href="/wiki/Paul_Ehrlich" title="Paul Ehrlich">Paul Ehrlich</a>, received the 1936 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Medicine" title="Nobel Prize in Medicine">Nobel Prize in Medicine</a> for his work on the chemical transmission of neural impulses.  Dale served as a pharmacologist and then as Director of the Wellcome Physiological Research Laboratories from 1904 to 1914, and later served as Trustee and Chairman of the Board of the <a href="/wiki/Wellcome_Trust" title="Wellcome Trust">Wellcome Trust</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[79]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/John_Vane" title="John Vane">John Vane</a> of Wellcome Research Laboratories shared the 1982 Nobel Prize for Medicine for his work on prostaglandin biology and the discovery of <a href="/wiki/Prostacyclin" title="Prostacyclin">prostacyclin</a>.  Vane served as Group Research and Development Director for The Wellcome Foundation from 1973 to 1985.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[80]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Gertrude_B._Elion" title="Gertrude B. Elion">Gertrude B. Elion</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_Hitchings" title="George Hitchings">George Hitchings</a>, both of the Wellcome Research Laboratories, shared the 1988 Nobel Prize in Medicine with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_James_W._Black" title="Sir James W. Black">Sir James W. Black</a> ""for their discoveries of important principles for drug treatment"." Elion and Hitchings were responsible for the discovery of a plethora of important drugs, including mercaptopurine<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pubs.acs.org_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-pubs.acs.org-65">[64]</a></sup> and thioguanine<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ScienceHistory_66-2"><a href="#cite_note-ScienceHistory-66">[65]</a></sup> for the treatment of leukemia, the immunosuppressant azothioprine,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[81]</a></sup> allopurinol for gout,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_purine_path_to_chemotherapy_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-The_purine_path_to_chemotherapy-67">[66]</a></sup> pyrimethamine for malaria,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes.com_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes.com-68">[67]</a></sup> the antibacterial trimethoprim,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ScienceHistory_66-3"><a href="#cite_note-ScienceHistory-66">[65]</a></sup> acyclovir for herpes virus infection,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[82]</a></sup> and nelarabine for cancer treatment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Philanthropy_and_social_responsibility">Philanthropy and social responsibility</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=GlaxoSmithKline&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Philanthropy and social responsibility">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GlaxoSmithKline_3_Franklin_Plaza_Philadelphia.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1992" data-file-width="2744" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/GlaxoSmithKline_3_Franklin_Plaza_Philadelphia.jpg/220px-GlaxoSmithKline_3_Franklin_Plaza_Philadelphia.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/GlaxoSmithKline_3_Franklin_Plaza_Philadelphia.jpg/330px-GlaxoSmithKline_3_Franklin_Plaza_Philadelphia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/GlaxoSmithKline_3_Franklin_Plaza_Philadelphia.jpg/440px-GlaxoSmithKline_3_Franklin_Plaza_Philadelphia.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GlaxoSmithKline_3_Franklin_Plaza_Philadelphia.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>GlaxoSmithKline, <a href="/wiki/Center_City,_Philadelphia" title="Center City, Philadelphia">Center City, Philadelphia</a></div></div></div>
<p>Since 2010, GlaxoSmithKline has several times ranked first among pharmaceutical companies on the Global Access to Medicines Index, which is funded by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bill_and_Melinda_Gates_Foundation" title="Bill and Melinda Gates Foundation">Bill and Melinda Gates Foundation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-www.accesstomedicineindex.org_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-www.accesstomedicineindex.org-85">[84]</a></sup> In 2014 the <a href="/wiki/Human_Rights_Campaign" title="Human Rights Campaign">Human Rights Campaign</a>, an <a href="/wiki/LGBT" title="LGBT">LGBT</a>-rights advocacy group gave GSK a score of 100 percent in its <a href="/wiki/Corporate_Equality_Index" title="Corporate Equality Index">Corporate Equality Index</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>GSK has been active, with the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> (WHO), in the Global Alliance to Eliminate Lymphatic Filariasis (GAELF). Around 120 million people globally are believed to be infected with <a href="/wiki/Filariasis" title="Filariasis">lymphatic filariasis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[86]</a></sup> In 2012 the company endorsed the <a href="/wiki/London_Declaration_on_Neglected_Tropical_Diseases" title="London Declaration on Neglected Tropical Diseases">London Declaration on Neglected Tropical Diseases</a>; it agreed to donate 400 million <a href="/wiki/Albendazole" title="Albendazole">albendazole</a> tablets to the WHO each year to fight <a href="/wiki/Soil-transmitted_helminthiasis" title="Soil-transmitted helminthiasis">soil-transmitted helminthiasis</a> and to provide 600 million albendazole tablets every year for lymphatic filariasis until the disease is eradicated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bill_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-bill-88">[87]</a></sup> As of 2014 over 5 billion treatments had been delivered, and 18 of 73 countries in which the disease is considered endemic had progressed to the surveillance stage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2009 the company said it would cut drug prices by 25 percent in 50 of the poorest nations, release intellectual property rights for substances and processes relevant to neglected disease into a <a href="/wiki/Patent_pool" title="Patent pool">patent pool</a> to encourage new drug development, and invest 20 percent of profits from the least-developed countries in medical infrastructure for those countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[90]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/M%C3%A9decins_Sans_Fronti%C3%A8res" title="Médecins Sans Frontières">Médecins Sans Frontières</a> welcomed the decision, but criticized GSK for failing to include HIV patents in its patent pool and for not including middle-income countries in the initiative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2013 GSK licensed its HIV portfolio to the Medicines Patent Pool for use in children, and agreed to negotiate a license for <a href="/wiki/Dolutegravir" title="Dolutegravir">dolutegravir</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Integrase_inhibitor" title="Integrase inhibitor">integrase inhibitor</a> then in clinical development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[92]</a></sup> In 2014 this license was extended to include dolutegravir and adults with HIV. The licenses include countries in which 93 percent of adults and 99 percent of children with HIV live.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[93]</a></sup> Also in 2013 GSK joined <a href="/wiki/AllTrials" title="AllTrials">AllTrials</a>, a British campaign to ensure that all clinical trials are registered and the results reported. The company said it would make its past clinical-trial reports available and future ones within a year of the studies' end.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[94]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversies">Controversies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=GlaxoSmithKline&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Controversies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2012_criminal_and_civil_settlement">2012 criminal and civil settlement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=GlaxoSmithKline&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: 2012 criminal and civil settlement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">Overview</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=GlaxoSmithKline&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Overview">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In July 2012 GSK pleaded guilty in the United States to criminal charges, and agreed to pay $3 billion, in what was the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Largest_Pharmaceutical_Settlements" title="List of Largest Pharmaceutical Settlements">largest settlement</a> until then between the Justice Department and a drug company. The $3 billion included a criminal fine of $956,814,400 and forfeiture of $43,185,600. The remaining $2 billion covered a civil settlement with the government under the <a href="/wiki/False_Claims_Act" title="False Claims Act">False Claims Act</a>. The investigation was launched largely on the basis of information from four whistleblowers who filed <a href="/wiki/Qui_tam" title="Qui tam">qui tam</a> (whistleblower) lawsuits against the company under the False Claims Act.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USDOJJuly2012_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-USDOJJuly2012-11">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>The charges stemmed from GSK's promotion of the anti-depressants Paxil (<a href="/wiki/Paroxetine" title="Paroxetine">paroxetine</a>) and Wellbutrin (<a href="/wiki/Bupropion" title="Bupropion">bupropion</a>) for unapproved uses from 1998–2003, specifically as suitable for patients under the age of 18, and from its failure to report safety data about Avandia (<a href="/wiki/Rosiglitazone" title="Rosiglitazone">rosiglitazone</a>), both in violation of the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Food,_Drug,_and_Cosmetic_Act" title="Federal Food, Drug, and Cosmetic Act">Federal Food, Drug, and Cosmetic Act</a>. Other drugs promoted for unapproved uses were two inhalers, Advair (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fluticasone/salmeterol" title="Fluticasone/salmeterol">fluticasone/salmeterol</a>) and Flovent (<a href="/wiki/Fluticasone_propionate" title="Fluticasone propionate">fluticasone propionate</a>), as well as Zofran (<a href="/wiki/Ondansetron" title="Ondansetron">ondansetron</a>), Imitrex (<a href="/wiki/Sumatriptan" title="Sumatriptan">sumatriptan</a>), Lotronex (<a href="/wiki/Alosetron" title="Alosetron">alosetron</a>) and Valtrex (<a href="/wiki/Valaciclovir" title="Valaciclovir">valaciclovir</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USDOJJuly2012_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-USDOJJuly2012-11">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>The settlement also covered reporting false best prices and underpaying rebates owed under the <a href="/wiki/Medicaid_Drug_Rebate_Program" title="Medicaid Drug Rebate Program">Medicaid Drug Rebate Program</a>, and kickbacks to physicians to prescribe GSK's drugs. There were all-expenses-paid spa treatments and hunting trips for doctors and their spouses, speakers' fees at conferences, and payment for articles <a href="/wiki/Medical_ghostwriter" title="Medical ghostwriter">ghostwritten</a> by the company and placed by physicians in medical journals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USDOJJuly2012_11-3"><a href="#cite_note-USDOJJuly2012-11">[10]</a></sup> The company set up a ghostwriting programme called CASPPER, initially to produce articles about Paxil but which was extended to cover Avandia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p>As part of the settlement GSK signed a five-year <a href="/wiki/Corporate_integrity_agreement" title="Corporate integrity agreement">corporate integrity agreement</a> with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Department_of_Health_and_Human_Services" title="Department of Health and Human Services">Department of Health and Human Services</a>, which obliged the company to make major changes in the way it did business, including changing its compensation programmes for its sales force and executives, and to implement and maintain transparency in its research practices and publication policies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USDOJJuly2012_11-4"><a href="#cite_note-USDOJJuly2012-11">[10]</a></sup> It announced in 2013 that it would no longer pay doctors to promote its drugs or attend medical conferences, and that its sales staff would no longer have prescription targets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reuters17Dec2013_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters17Dec2013-97">[96]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Rosiglitazone_.28Avandia.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Rosiglitazone_(Avandia)">Rosiglitazone (Avandia)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=GlaxoSmithKline&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Rosiglitazone (Avandia)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Rosiglitazone#Adverse_effects" title="Rosiglitazone">Rosiglitazone § Adverse effects</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rosiglitazone#Lawsuits" title="Rosiglitazone">Rosiglitazone § Lawsuits</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Avandia_2mg_oral_tablet.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="840" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Avandia_2mg_oral_tablet.jpg/220px-Avandia_2mg_oral_tablet.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Avandia_2mg_oral_tablet.jpg/330px-Avandia_2mg_oral_tablet.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Avandia_2mg_oral_tablet.jpg/440px-Avandia_2mg_oral_tablet.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Avandia_2mg_oral_tablet.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Rosiglitazone" title="Rosiglitazone">Rosiglitazone</a></div></div></div>
<p>The 2012 settlement included a criminal fine of $242,612,800 for failing to report safety data to the FDA about Avandia (<a href="/wiki/Rosiglitazone" title="Rosiglitazone">rosiglitazone</a>), a <a href="/wiki/Diabetes_mellitus_type_2" title="Diabetes mellitus type 2">diabetes</a> drug approved in 1999, and a civil settlement of $657 million for making false claims about it. The Justice Department said GSK had promoted rosiglitazone to physicians with misleading information, including that it conferred cardiovascular benefits despite an FDA-mandated label warning of cardiovascular risks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USDOJJuly2012_11-5"><a href="#cite_note-USDOJJuly2012-11">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1999 <a href="/wiki/John_Buse" title="John Buse">John Buse</a>, a diabetes specialist, told medical conferences that rosiglitazone might carry an increased risk of cardiovascular problems. GSK threatened to sue him, called his university head of department, and persuaded him to sign a retraction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[97]</a></sup> GSK raised questions internally about the drug's safety in 2000, and in 2002 the company <a href="/wiki/Medical_ghostwriter" title="Medical ghostwriter">ghostwrote</a> an article in <a href="/wiki/Circulation_(journal)" title="Circulation (journal)"><i>Circulation</i></a> describing a GSK funded clinical trial that suggested rosiglitazone might have a beneficial effect on cardiovascular risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[98]</a></sup> From 2001 reports began to link the <a href="/wiki/Thiazolidinedione" title="Thiazolidinedione">thiazolidinediones</a> (the class of drugs to which rosiglitazone belongs) to <a href="/wiki/Heart_failure" title="Heart failure">heart failure</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid20154334_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid20154334-100">[99]</a></sup> In April that year GSK began a six-year, <a href="/wiki/Open-label_trial" title="Open-label trial">open-label</a>, <a href="/wiki/Randomized_controlled_trial" title="Randomized controlled trial">randomized trial</a>, known as RECORD, to examine rosiglitazone and cardiovascular events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RECORDLancet_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-RECORDLancet-101">[100]</a></sup> Two GSK <a href="/wiki/Meta-analysis" title="Meta-analysis">meta-analyses</a> in 2005 and 2006 showed an increased risk of cardiovascular problems with rosiglitazone; the information was passed to the FDA and posted on the company website, but not otherwise published. By December 2006 rosiglitazone had become the top-selling diabetes drug, with annual sales of US$3.3 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid20154334_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid20154334-100">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>In June 2007 the <i>New England Journal of Medicine</i> published a meta-analysis that associated the drug with an increased risk of <a href="/wiki/Myocardial_infarction" title="Myocardial infarction">heart attack</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[101]</a></sup> GSK had reportedly tried to persuade one of the authors, <a href="/wiki/Steven_Nissen" title="Steven Nissen">Steven Nissen</a>, not to publish it, after receiving an advance copy from one of the journal's peer reviewers, a GSK consultant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[102]</a></sup> In July 2007 FDA scientists suggested that rosiglitazone had caused 83,000 excess heart attacks between 1999 and 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Senatereport_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-Senatereport-104">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>4</span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graham_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Graham-105">[104]</a></sup> The FDA placed restrictions on the drug, including adding a <a href="/wiki/Boxed_warning" title="Boxed warning">boxed warning</a>, but did not withdraw it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[105]</a></sup> (In 2013 the FDA rejected that the drug had caused excess heart attacks.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FDAheart_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FDAheart-107">[106]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate_Committee_on_Finance" title="United States Senate Committee on Finance">Senate Finance Committee</a> inquiry concluded in 2010 that GSK had sought to intimidate scientists who had concerns about rosiglitazone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Senatereport_104-1"><a href="#cite_note-Senatereport-104">[103]</a></sup> In February that year the company tried to halt publication of an editorial about the controversy by Nissen in the <i>European Heart Journal</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[107]</a></sup>
</p><p>The results of GSK's RECORD trial were published in June 2009. It confirmed an association between rosiglitazone and an increased risk of heart failure and fractures, but not of heart attack, and concluded that it "does not increase the risk of overall cardiovascular morbidity or mortality compared with standard glucose-lowering drugs."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RECORDLancet_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-RECORDLancet-101">[100]</a></sup> Steven Nissan and Kathy Wolkski argued that the study's low event rates reduced its statistical power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[108]</a></sup> In September 2009 rosiglitazone was suspended in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[109]</a></sup> The results of the RECORD study were confirmed in 2013 by the Duke Clinical Research Institute, in an independent review required by the FDA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[110]</a></sup> In November that year the FDA lifted the restrictions it had placed on the drug.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[111]</a></sup> The boxed warning about heart attack was removed; the warning about heart failure remained in place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FDAheart_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-FDAheart-107">[106]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Paroxetine_.28Paxil.2FSeroxat.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Paroxetine_(Paxil/Seroxat)">Paroxetine (Paxil/Seroxat)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=GlaxoSmithKline&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Paroxetine (Paxil/Seroxat)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Study_329" title="Study 329">Study 329</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Paroxetine_pill.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="900" data-file-width="1200" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Paroxetine_pill.jpg/220px-Paroxetine_pill.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Paroxetine_pill.jpg/330px-Paroxetine_pill.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Paroxetine_pill.jpg/440px-Paroxetine_pill.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Paroxetine_pill.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Paroxetine" title="Paroxetine">Paroxetine</a>, known as Paxil and Seroxat</div></div></div>
<p>GSK was fined for promoting Paxil/Seroxat (<a href="/wiki/Paroxetine" title="Paroxetine">paroxetine</a>) for treating depression in the under-18s, although the drug had not been approved for pediatric use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USDOJJuly2012_11-6"><a href="#cite_note-USDOJJuly2012-11">[10]</a></sup>
Paxil had $4.97 billion worldwide sales in 2003.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CMAJ2004_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-CMAJ2004-113">[112]</a></sup> The company conducted nine clinical trials between 1994 and 2002, none of which showed that Paxil helped children with depression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[113]</a></sup> From 1998 to 2003 it promoted the drug for the under-18s, paying physicians to go on all-expenses paid trips, five-star hotels and spas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USDOJJuly2012_11-7"><a href="#cite_note-USDOJJuly2012-11">[10]</a></sup> From 2004 Paxil's label, along with those of similar drugs, included an FDA-mandated boxed warning that it might increase the risk of suicidal ideation and behaviour in patients under 18.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USDOJJuly2012_11-8"><a href="#cite_note-USDOJJuly2012-11">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>An internal SmithKline Beecham document said in 1998, about withheld data from two GSK studies: "It would be commercially unacceptable to include a statement that [pediatric] efficacy had not been demonstrated, as this would undermine the profile of paroxetine."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CMAJ2004_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-CMAJ2004-113">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[114]</a></sup> The company <a href="/wiki/Medical_ghostwriter" title="Medical ghostwriter">ghostwrote</a> an article, published in 2001 in the <i>Journal of the American Academy of Child and Adolescent Psychiatry</i>, that misreported the results of one of its clinical trials, <a href="/wiki/Study_329" title="Study 329">Study 329</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USDOJJuly2012_11-9"><a href="#cite_note-USDOJJuly2012-11">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[115]</a></sup> The article concluded that Paxil was "generally well tolerated and effective for major depression in adolescents."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[116]</a></sup> The suppression of the research findings is the subject of <i><a href="/wiki/Side_Effects_(Bass_book)" title="Side Effects (Bass book)">Side Effects</a></i> (2008) by <a href="/wiki/Alison_Bass" title="Alison Bass">Alison Bass</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[118]</a></sup>
</p><p>For 10 years GSK marketed Paxil as non-habit forming. In 2001 35 patients filed a class-action suit alleging they had suffered withdrawal symptoms, and in 2002, a Los Angeles court issued an injunction preventing GSK from advertising that the drug was not habit forming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[119]</a></sup> The court withdrew the injunction after the FDA objected that the court had no jurisdiction over drug marketing that the FDA had approved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[120]</a></sup> In 2003, a World Health Organization committee reported that Paxil was among the top 30 drugs, and top three antidepressants, for which dependence had been reported.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHOdependence_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHOdependence-122">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[n 2]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Bupropion_.28Wellbutrin.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Bupropion_(Wellbutrin)">Bupropion (Wellbutrin)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=GlaxoSmithKline&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Bupropion (Wellbutrin)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The company was also fined for promoting Wellbutrin (<a href="/wiki/Bupropion" title="Bupropion">bupropion</a>) – approved at the time for <a href="/wiki/Major_depressive_disorder" title="Major depressive disorder">major depressive disorder</a> and also sold as a smoking-cessation aid, Zyban – for weight loss and the treatment of <a href="/wiki/Attention_deficit_hyperactivity_disorder" title="Attention deficit hyperactivity disorder">attention deficit hyperactivity disorder</a>, sexual dysfunction and substance addiction. GSK paid doctors to promote these off-label uses, and set up supposedly independent advisory boards and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Continuing_Medical_Education" title="Continuing Medical Education">Continuing Medical Education</a> programmes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USDOJJuly2012_11-10"><a href="#cite_note-USDOJJuly2012-11">[10]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Antitrust_case_over_griseofulvin">Antitrust case over griseofulvin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=GlaxoSmithKline&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Antitrust case over griseofulvin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the 1960s Glaxo Group Ltd. (Glaxo) and Imperial Chemical Industries (ICI) each owned patents covering various aspects of the antifungal drug <a href="/wiki/Griseofulvin" title="Griseofulvin">griseofulvin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GSKICI_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-GSKICI-124">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>54, nn. 1–2</span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LaHatte_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-LaHatte-125">[123]</a></sup> They created a <a href="/wiki/Patent_pool" title="Patent pool">patent pool</a> by <a href="/wiki/Cross-licensing" title="Cross-licensing">cross-licensing</a> their patents, subject to express licensing restrictions that the chemical from which the "finished" form of the drug (tablets and capsules) was made must not be resold in bulk form, and they licensed other drug companies to sell the drug in finished form and subject to similar restrictions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GSKICI_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-GSKICI-124">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>54–55</span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LaHatte_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-LaHatte-125">[123]</a></sup> The effect and intent of the bulk-sale restriction was to keep the drug chemical out of the hands of small companies that might act as price-cutters, and the effect was to maintain stable, uniform prices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leslie_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-Leslie-126">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jacobson_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jacobson-128">[126]</a></sup>
</p><p>The United States brought an antitrust suit against the two companies—<i><a href="/wiki/United_States_v._Glaxo_Group_Ltd." title="United States v. Glaxo Group Ltd.">United States v. Glaxo Group Ltd.</a></i>—charging them with violation of the Sherman Act and also seeking to have the patents declared invalid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GSKICI_124-2"><a href="#cite_note-GSKICI-124">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>55</span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LaHatte_125-2"><a href="#cite_note-LaHatte-125">[123]</a></sup> The trial court found that the defendants had engaged in several unlawful conspiracies, but dismissed the part of the suit seeking invalidation of patents and refused to grant as relief mandatory sales of the bulk drug chemical and compulsory licensing of the patents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GSKICI_124-3"><a href="#cite_note-GSKICI-124">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>56</span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LaHatte_125-3"><a href="#cite_note-LaHatte-125">[123]</a></sup> The government appealed to the Supreme Court, which reversed, in <i><a href="/wiki/United_States_v._Glaxo_Group_Ltd." title="United States v. Glaxo Group Ltd.">United States v. Glaxo Group Ltd.</a></i>, 410 U.S. 52 (1973).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LaHatte_125-4"><a href="#cite_note-LaHatte-125">[123]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ribena">Ribena</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=GlaxoSmithKline&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Ribena">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:224px;max-width:224px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ribena_bottle_(front_label_closeup).jpg"><img alt="photograph" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4000" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Ribena_bottle_%28front_label_closeup%29.jpg/220px-Ribena_bottle_%28front_label_closeup%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Ribena_bottle_%28front_label_closeup%29.jpg/330px-Ribena_bottle_%28front_label_closeup%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Ribena_bottle_%28front_label_closeup%29.jpg/440px-Ribena_bottle_%28front_label_closeup%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ribena_bottle_(back_label_closeup).jpg"><img alt="photograph" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4000" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Ribena_bottle_%28back_label_closeup%29.jpg/220px-Ribena_bottle_%28back_label_closeup%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Ribena_bottle_%28back_label_closeup%29.jpg/330px-Ribena_bottle_%28back_label_closeup%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Ribena_bottle_%28back_label_closeup%29.jpg/440px-Ribena_bottle_%28back_label_closeup%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Old Ribena bottle, year unknown, made by Beecham Products, Brentford, Middlesex; the label states: "widely used in hospitals and clinics."</div></div></div></div>
<p>There were concerns in the 2000s about the sugar and vitamin content of <a href="/wiki/Ribena" title="Ribena">Ribena</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Blackcurrant" title="Blackcurrant">blackcurrant</a>-based <a href="/wiki/Syrup" title="Syrup">syrup</a> and <a href="/wiki/Soft_drink" title="Soft drink">soft drink</a> owned by GSK until 2013. Produced in England by H.W. Carter &amp; Co from the 1930s, the company's unbranded syrup was distributed to children as a source of <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_C" title="Vitamin C">vitamin C</a> during World War II, which gave the drink a reputation as good for health. <a href="/wiki/Beecham_Group" title="Beecham Group">Beecham</a> bought H. W. Carter in 1955.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[127]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2001 the British <a href="/wiki/Advertising_Standards_Authority_(United_Kingdom)" title="Advertising Standards Authority (United Kingdom)">Advertising Standards Authority</a> (ASA) required GSK to withdraw its claim that Ribena Toothkind, a lower-sugar variety, did not encourage tooth decay. A company poster showed bottles of Toothkind in place of the bristles on a toothbrush. The ASA's ruling was upheld by the High Court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[128]</a></sup> In 2007 GSK was fined $217,000 in New Zealand over its claim that ready-to-drink Ribena contained high levels of vitamin C, after it was found to contain no detectable vitamin C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[129]</a></sup> In 2013 GSK sold Ribena and another drink, <a href="/wiki/Lucozade" title="Lucozade">Lucozade</a>, to the Japanese multinational <a href="/wiki/Suntory" title="Suntory">Suntory</a> for £1.35 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Monaghan_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-Monaghan-76">[75]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="SB_Pharmco_Puerto_Rico">SB Pharmco Puerto Rico</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=GlaxoSmithKline&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: SB Pharmco Puerto Rico">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2010 the US Department of Justice announced that GSK would pay a $150 million criminal fine and forfeiture, and a civil settlement of $600 million under the False Claims Act. The fines stemmed from production of improperly made and adulterated drugs from 2001 to 2005 at GSK's subsidiary, SB Pharmco Puerto Rico Inc., in Cidra, Puerto Rico, which at the time produced $5.5 billion of products each year. The drugs involved were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kytril" title="Kytril">Kytril</a>, an antiemetic; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bactroban" title="Bactroban">Bactroban</a>, used to treat skin infections; Paxil, the anti-depressant; and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Avandamet" title="Avandamet">Avandamet</a>, a diabetes drug.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[130]</a></sup> GSK closed the factory in 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTPuerto_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYTPuerto-133">[131]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the <i>New York Times</i>, the case began in 2002 when GSK sent experts to fix problems cited by the FDA. The lead inspector recommended recalls of defective products, but they were not authorised; she was fired in 2003 and filed a whistleblower lawsuit. In 2005 federal marshals seized $2 billion worth of products, the largest such seizure in history. In the 2010 settlement SB Pharmco plead guilty to criminal charges, and agreed to pay $150 million in a criminal fine and forfeiture, at that time the largest such payment ever by a manufacturer of adulterated drugs, and $600 million in civil penalties to settle the civil lawsuit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTPuerto_133-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYTPuerto-133">[131]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="China">China</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=GlaxoSmithKline&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: China">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2013 Chinese authorities announced that, since 2007, GSK had funnelled HK$3.8 billion in kickbacks to GSK managers, doctors, hospitals and others who prescribed their drugs, using over 700 travel agencies and consulting firms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[132]</a></sup> Chinese authorities arrested four GSK executives as part of a four-month investigation into claims that doctors were bribed with cash and sexual favours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[133]</a></sup> In 2014 a Chinese court found the company guilty of bribery and imposed a fine of $490 million. Mark Reilly, the British head of GSK's Chinese operations, received a three-year suspended prison sentence after a one-day trial held in secret.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[134]</a></sup> Reilly was reportedly deported from China and dismissed by the company.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[135]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Market_manipulation_in_the_UK">Market manipulation in the UK</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=GlaxoSmithKline&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Market manipulation in the UK">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In February 2016 the company was fined over £37 million in the UK by the <a href="/wiki/Competition_and_Markets_Authority" title="Competition and Markets Authority">Competition and Markets Authority</a> for paying <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Generics_UK&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Generics UK (page does not exist)">Generics UK</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alpharma" title="Alpharma">Alpharma</a> and <a href="/wiki/Norton_Healthcare" title="Norton Healthcare">Norton Healthcare</a> more than £50m between 2001 and 2004 to keep generic varieties of <a href="/wiki/Paroxetine" title="Paroxetine">paroxetine</a> out of the UK market. The generics companies were fined a further £8 million. At the end of 2003, when generics became available in the UK, the price of paroxetine dropped by 70 percent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[136]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Miscellaneous">Miscellaneous</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=GlaxoSmithKline&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Miscellaneous">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Italian police sought bribery charges in May 2004 against 4,400 doctors and 273 GSK employees. GSK and its predecessor were accused of having spent £152m on physicians, pharmacists and others, giving them cameras, computers, holidays and cash. Doctors were alleged to have received cash based on the number of patients they treated with a cancer drug, <a href="/wiki/Topotecan" title="Topotecan">topotecan</a> (Hycamtin).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[137]</a></sup> The following month prosecutors in Munich accused 70–100 doctors of having accepted bribes from SmithKline Beecham between 1997 and 1999. The inquiry was opened over allegations that the company had given over 4,000 hospital doctors money and free trips.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[138]</a></sup> All charges were dismissed by the Verona court in January 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[139]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2006 in the United States GSK settled the largest tax dispute in IRS history, agreeing to pay $3.1 billion. At issue were Zantac and other products sold in 1989–2005. The case revolved around intracompany <a href="/wiki/Transfer_pricing" title="Transfer pricing">transfer pricing</a>—determining the share of profit attributable to the US subsidiaries of GSK and subject to tax by the IRS.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[140]</a></sup>
</p><p>The UK's <a href="/wiki/Serious_Fraud_Office_(United_Kingdom)" title="Serious Fraud Office (United Kingdom)">Serious Fraud Office</a> (SFO) opened a criminal inquiry in 2014 into GSK's sales practices, using powers granted by the <a href="/wiki/Bribery_Act_2010" title="Bribery Act 2010">Bribery Act 2010</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[141]</a></sup> The SFO said it was collaborating with Chinese authorities to investigate bringing charges in the UK related to GSK's activities in China, Europe and the Middle East.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[142]</a></sup> Also as of 2014 the US Department of Justice was investigating GSK with reference to the <a href="/wiki/Foreign_Corrupt_Practices_Act" title="Foreign Corrupt Practices Act">Foreign Corrupt Practices Act</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[143]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Acquisition-history_diagram">Acquisition-history diagram</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=GlaxoSmithKline&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Acquisition-history diagram">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=GlaxoSmithKline&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-4">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Glaxo Wellcome was formed from Glaxo's 1995 acquisition of The Wellcome Foundation and SmithKline Beecham from the 1989 merger of the <a href="/wiki/Beecham_Group" title="Beecham Group">Beecham Group</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Smith,_Kline_%26_French" title="Smith, Kline &amp; French">SmithKline Beckman Corporation</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-123"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-123">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> Expert Committee on Drug Dependence, 2003: "The Committee noted the striking number of reports on paroxetine and 'withdrawal syndrome' ... The representative of Consumers International reported that a number of patients had experienced difficulty in withdrawing from SSRIs in general. It was agreed that withdrawal was indeed a problem in some patients, but there was a difference of opinion on the degree of dependence that was involved, given the possibility that the need for treatment of resistant or relapsing disease could make these drugs indispensable for patient care. The Committee expressed concern about the possibility of inappropriate prescribing resulting in the risk of problems of withdrawal outweighing the benefits of treatment with SSRIs."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHOdependence_122-1"><a href="#cite_note-WHOdependence-122">[121]</a></sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
